<ul><li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="185" data-file-width="180" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/16px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/23px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/31px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" title="Category" width="16"/> <a href="/wiki/Category:Physical_cosmology" title="Category:Physical cosmology">Category</a></li>

portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Physical_cosmology" title="Template:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Physical_cosmology" title="Template talk:Physical cosmology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Physical_cosmology&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Galileo di Vincenzo Bonaiuti de' Galilei</b> (15 February 1564 – 8 January 1642) was an Italian <a href="/wiki/Astronomer" title="Astronomer">astronomer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Engineer" title="Engineer">engineer</a>, sometimes described as a <a href="/wiki/Polymath" title="Polymath">polymath</a>. Commonly referred to as <b>Galileo</b>, his name was pronounced <span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span><span class="wrap"> </span><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/æ/: 'a' in 'bad'">æ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span></span>/</a></span></span> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">GAL</span>-ih-<span style="font-size:90%">LAY</span>-oh <span style="font-size:90%">GAL</span>-ih-<span style="font-size:90%">LAY</span>-ee</i></a>, <small>Italian: </small><span class="IPA" lang="it-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Italian" title="Help:IPA/Italian">[ɡaliˈlɛːo ɡaliˈlɛi]</a></span>). He was born in the city of <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a>, then part of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Florence" title="Duchy of Florence">Duchy of Florence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Galileo has been called the "father" of <a href="/wiki/Observational_astronomy" title="Observational astronomy">observational astronomy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> modern physics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Einstein_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-Einstein-7">[7]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_science" title="Modern science">modern science</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo studied <a href="/wiki/Speed" title="Speed">speed</a> and <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">velocity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">gravity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">free fall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Principle_of_relativity" title="Principle of relativity">principle of relativity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">inertia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Projectile_motion" title="Projectile motion">projectile motion</a> and also worked in applied science and technology, describing the properties of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pendulums" title="Pendulums">pendulums</a> and "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrostatic" title="Hydrostatic">hydrostatic</a> balances". He invented the <a href="/wiki/Thermoscope" title="Thermoscope">thermoscope</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Sector_(instrument)" title="Sector (instrument)">military compasses</a>, and used the <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescope</a> for scientific observations of celestial objects. His contributions to observational astronomy include telescopic confirmation of the <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_Venus" title="Phases of Venus">phases of Venus</a>, observation of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_Moons" title="Galilean Moons">four largest satellites</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a>, observation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_rings" title="Saturn's rings">Saturn's rings</a>, and analysis of <a href="/wiki/Lunar_craters" title="Lunar craters">lunar craters</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunspots" title="Sunspots">sunspots</a>.
</p><p>Galileo's championing of <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">Copernican heliocentrism</a> (Earth rotating daily and revolving around the sun) was met with opposition from within the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> and from some astronomers. The matter was investigated by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Inquisition" title="Roman Inquisition">Roman Inquisition</a> in 1615, which concluded that heliocentrism was foolish, absurd, and heretical since it contradicted Holy Scripture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannam2009329–344_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannam2009329–344-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994127–131_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994127–131-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro201074_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro201074-12">[12]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo later defended his views in <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i> (1632), which appeared to attack <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_VIII" title="Pope Urban VIII">Pope Urban VIII</a> and thus alienated both the Pope and the <a href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a>, who had both supported Galileo up until this point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannam2009329–344_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannam2009329–344-10">[10]</a></sup> He was tried by the Inquisition, found "vehemently suspect of heresy", and forced to recant. He spent the rest of his life under house arrest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro199747_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro199747-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHilliam200596_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHilliam200596-14">[14]</a></sup> During this time, he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Two_New_Sciences" title="Two New Sciences">Two New Sciences</a></i> (1638), primarily concerning <a href="/wiki/Kinematics" title="Kinematics">kinematics</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Strength_of_materials" title="Strength of materials">strength of materials</a>, summarizing work he had done around forty years earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JoCarney_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-JoCarney-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Death"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Death</span></a></li>

</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span></h2>
<p>Galileo was born in <a href="/wiki/Pisa" title="Pisa">Pisa</a> (then part of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Florence" title="Duchy of Florence">Duchy of Florence</a>), Italy, on 15 February 1564,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galileo_Galilei_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Galileo_Galilei-16">[16]</a></sup> the first of six children of <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Galilei" title="Vincenzo Galilei">Vincenzo Galilei</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutenist" title="Lutenist">lutenist</a>, composer, and <a href="/wiki/Music_theory" title="Music theory">music theorist</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giulia_Ammannati" title="Giulia Ammannati">Giulia Ammannati</a>, who had married in 1562. Galileo became an accomplished lutenist himself and would have learned early from his father a scepticism for established authority.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGribbin200826_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGribbin200826-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Three of Galileo's five siblings survived infancy. The youngest, <a href="/wiki/Michelagnolo_Galilei" title="Michelagnolo Galilei">Michelangelo</a> (or Michelagnolo), also became a lutenist and composer who added to Galileo's financial burdens for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGribbin200830_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGribbin200830-18">[18]</a></sup> Michelangelo was unable to contribute his fair share of their father's promised dowries to their brothers-in-law, who would later attempt to seek legal remedies for payments due. Michelangelo would also occasionally have to borrow funds from Galileo to support his musical endeavours and excursions. These financial burdens may have contributed to Galileo's early desire to develop inventions that would bring him additional income.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGribbin200831_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGribbin200831-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>When Galileo Galilei was eight, his family moved to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florence,_Italy" title="Florence, Italy">Florence</a>, but he was left under the care of Muzio Tedaldi for two years. When Galileo was ten, he left Pisa to join his family in Florence and there he was under the tutelage of Jacopo Borghini.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Galileo_Galilei_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Galileo_Galilei-16">[16]</a></sup> He was educated, particularly in logic, from 1575 to 1578 in the <a href="/wiki/Vallombrosa_Abbey" title="Vallombrosa Abbey">Vallombrosa Abbey</a>, about 30 km southeast of Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert,_N._W._1963_223–231_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert,_N._W._1963_223–231-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h3>
<p>Galileo tended to refer to himself only by his given name. At the time, surnames were optional in Italy, and his given name had the same origin as his sometimes-family name, Galilei. Both his given and family name ultimately derive from an ancestor, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Bonaiuti" title="Galileo Bonaiuti">Galileo Bonaiuti</a>, an important physician, professor, and politician in Florence in the 15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel200016_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel200016-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Galileo Bonaiuti was buried in the same church, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce in Florence</a>, where about 200 years later, Galileo Galilei was also buried.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>When he did refer to himself with more than one name, it was sometimes as Galileo Galilei Linceo, a reference to his being a member of the <a href="/wiki/Accademia_dei_Lincei" title="Accademia dei Lincei">Accademia dei Lincei</a>, an elite pro-science organization in Italy. It was common for mid-sixteenth-century Tuscan families to name the eldest son after the parents' surname.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel200013_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel200013-25">[25]</a></sup> Hence, Galileo Galilei was not necessarily named after his ancestor Galileo Bonaiuti. The Italian male given name "Galileo" (and thence the surname "Galilei") derives from the Latin "Galilaeus", meaning "of <a href="/wiki/Galilee" title="Galilee">Galilee</a>", a biblically significant region in Northern <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel200016_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel200016-22">[22]</a></sup> Because of that region, the adjective <i>galilaios</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> Γαλιλαῖος, <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>Galilaeus</i>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a> <i>Galileo</i>), which means "Galilean", was used in antiquity (particularly by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Julian" title="Emperor Julian">emperor Julian</a>) to refer to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christ" title="Christ">Christ</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">his followers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>The biblical roots of Galileo's name and surname were to become the subject of a famous pun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989300,_330_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989300,_330-28">[28]</a></sup> In 1614, during the <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">Galileo affair</a>, one of Galileo's opponents, the Dominican priest <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_Caccini" title="Tommaso Caccini">Tommaso Caccini</a>, delivered against Galileo a controversial and influential <a href="/wiki/Tommaso_Caccini#Sermon_at_Santa_Maria_Novella" title="Tommaso Caccini">sermon</a>. In it he made a point of quoting <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">Acts</a> <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Acts+1%3A11&amp;version=KJV" rel="nofollow">1:11</a></span>, "Ye men of Galilee, why stand ye gazing up into heaven?" (in the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> version found in the <a href="/wiki/Vulgate" title="Vulgate">Vulgate</a>: <i>Viri Galilaei, quid statis aspicientes in caelum?</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naess_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-naess-29">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:A_nun,_traditionally_identified_as_Suor_Maria_Celeste,_daugh_Wellcome_L0031890.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3864" data-file-width="2781" decoding="async" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/A_nun%2C_traditionally_identified_as_Suor_Maria_Celeste%2C_daugh_Wellcome_L0031890.jpg/170px-A_nun%2C_traditionally_identified_as_Suor_Maria_Celeste%2C_daugh_Wellcome_L0031890.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/A_nun%2C_traditionally_identified_as_Suor_Maria_Celeste%2C_daugh_Wellcome_L0031890.jpg/255px-A_nun%2C_traditionally_identified_as_Suor_Maria_Celeste%2C_daugh_Wellcome_L0031890.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/A_nun%2C_traditionally_identified_as_Suor_Maria_Celeste%2C_daugh_Wellcome_L0031890.jpg/340px-A_nun%2C_traditionally_identified_as_Suor_Maria_Celeste%2C_daugh_Wellcome_L0031890.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:A_nun,_traditionally_identified_as_Suor_Maria_Celeste,_daugh_Wellcome_L0031890.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait believed to be of Galileo's elder daughter <a href="/wiki/Maria_Celeste" title="Maria Celeste">Virginia</a>, who was particularly devoted to her father</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span></h3>
<p>Despite being a genuinely pious Roman Catholic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt199417,_213_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt199417,_213-30">[30]</a></sup> Galileo fathered three children out of wedlock with <a href="/wiki/Marina_Gamba" title="Marina Gamba">Marina Gamba</a>. They had two daughters, Virginia (born 1600) and Livia (born 1601), and a son, <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Gamba" title="Vincenzo Gamba">Vincenzo</a> (born 1606).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RosenGothard2009_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-RosenGothard2009-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Due to their illegitimate birth, Galileo considered the girls unmarriageable, if not posing problems of prohibitively expensive support or dowries, which would have been similar to Galileo's previous extensive financial problems with two of his sisters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGribbin200842_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGribbin200842-32">[32]</a></sup> Their only worthy alternative was the religious life. Both girls were accepted by the convent of San Matteo in <a href="/wiki/Arcetri" title="Arcetri">Arcetri</a> and remained there for the rest of their lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel20005_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel20005-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>Virginia took the name <a href="/wiki/Maria_Celeste" title="Maria Celeste">Maria Celeste</a> upon entering the convent. She died on 2 April 1634, and is buried with Galileo at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilica_of_Santa_Croce,_Florence" title="Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence">Basilica of Santa Croce, Florence</a>. Livia took the name Sister Arcangela and was ill for most of her life. Vincenzo was later <a href="/wiki/Legitimation" title="Legitimation">legitimised</a> as the legal heir of Galileo and married Sestilia Bocchineri.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Career_as_a_scientist">Career as a scientist</span></h2>
<p>Although Galileo seriously considered the priesthood as a young man, at his father's urging he instead enrolled in 1580 at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Pisa" title="University of Pisa">University of Pisa</a> for a medical degree.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReston20003–14_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReston20003–14-35">[35]</a></sup> He was influenced by the lectures of <a href="/wiki/Girolamo_Borro" title="Girolamo Borro">Girolamo Borro</a> and Francesco Buonamici of Florence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gilbert,_N._W._1963_223–231_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gilbert,_N._W._1963_223–231-21">[21]</a></sup> In 1581, when he was studying medicine, he noticed a swinging <a href="/wiki/Chandelier" title="Chandelier">chandelier</a>, which air currents shifted about to swing in larger and smaller arcs. To him, it seemed, by comparison with his heartbeat, that the chandelier took the same amount of time to swing back and forth, no matter how far it was swinging. When he returned home, he set up two <a href="/wiki/Pendulum" title="Pendulum">pendulums</a> of equal length and swung one with a large sweep and the other with a small sweep and found that they kept time together. It was not until the work of <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>, almost one hundred years later, that the <a href="/wiki/Tautochrone_curve" title="Tautochrone curve">tautochrone</a> nature of a swinging pendulum was used to create an accurate timepiece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asimov_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asimov-36">[36]</a></sup> Up to this point, Galileo had deliberately been kept away from mathematics, since a physician earned a higher income than a mathematician. However, after accidentally attending a lecture on geometry, he talked his reluctant father into letting him study mathematics and <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a> instead of medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asimov_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asimov-36">[36]</a></sup> He created a <a href="/wiki/Thermoscope" title="Thermoscope">thermoscope</a>, a forerunner of the <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometer</a>, and, in 1586, published a small book on the design of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrostatic" title="Hydrostatic">hydrostatic</a> balance he had invented (which first brought him to the attention of the scholarly world). Galileo also studied <i>disegno</i>, a term encompassing fine art, and, in 1588, obtained the position of instructor in the <a href="/wiki/Accademia_delle_Arti_del_Disegno" title="Accademia delle Arti del Disegno">Accademia delle Arti del Disegno</a> in Florence, teaching perspective and <a href="/wiki/Chiaroscuro" title="Chiaroscuro">chiaroscuro</a>. In the same year, upon invitation by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florentine_Academy" title="Florentine Academy">Florentine Academy</a>, he presented two lectures, <i><a href="/wiki/On_the_Shape,_Location,_and_Size_of_Dante%27s_Inferno" title="On the Shape, Location, and Size of Dante's Inferno">On the Shape, Location, and Size of Dante's Inferno</a></i>, in an attempt to propose a rigorous cosmological model of <a href="/wiki/Inferno_(Dante)" title="Inferno (Dante)">Dante's hell</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Being inspired by the artistic tradition of the city and the works of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_art" title="Renaissance art">Renaissance artists</a>, Galileo acquired an <a href="/wiki/Aestheticism" title="Aestheticism">aesthetic mentality</a>. While a young teacher at the Accademia, he began a lifelong friendship with the Florentine painter <a href="/wiki/Cigoli" title="Cigoli">Cigoli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-panofsky_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-panofsky-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1589, he was appointed to the chair of mathematics in Pisa. In 1591, his father died, and he was entrusted with the care of his younger brother <a href="/wiki/Michelagnolo_Galilei" title="Michelagnolo Galilei">Michelagnolo</a>. In 1592, he moved to the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Padua" title="University of Padua">University of Padua</a> where he taught geometry, <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">mechanics</a>, and astronomy until 1610.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt199445–66_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt199445–66-40">[40]</a></sup> During this period, Galileo made significant discoveries in both pure <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fundamental_science" title="Fundamental science">fundamental science</a> (for example, <a href="/wiki/Kinematics" title="Kinematics">kinematics</a> of motion and astronomy) as well as practical <a href="/wiki/Applied_science" title="Applied science">applied science</a> (for example, strength of materials and pioneering the telescope). His multiple interests included the study of <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">astrology</a>, which at the time was a discipline tied to the studies of mathematics and astronomy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astronomy">Astronomy</span></h3>
<h4><span id="Kepler.27s_supernova"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kepler's_supernova">Kepler's supernova</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">Tycho Brahe</a> and others had observed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supernova_of_1572" title="Supernova of 1572">supernova of 1572</a>. Ottavio Brenzoni's letter of 15 January 1605 to Galileo brought the 1572 supernova and the less bright nova of 1601 to Galileo's notice. Galileo observed and discussed <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_Supernova" title="Kepler's Supernova">Kepler's Supernova</a> in 1604. Since these new stars displayed no detectable <a href="/wiki/Parallax#Diurnal_parallax" title="Parallax">diurnal parallax</a>, Galileo concluded that they were distant stars, and, therefore, disproved the Aristotelian belief in the immutability of the heavens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Refracting_telescope">Refracting telescope</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_galilei,_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1470" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG/220px-Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG/330px-Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG/440px-Galileo_galilei%2C_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_galilei,_telescopi_del_1609-10_ca..JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galileo's "cannocchiali" <a href="/wiki/Telescope" title="Telescope">telescopes</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Museo_Galileo" title="Museo Galileo">Museo Galileo</a>, Florence</div></div></div>
<p>Based only on uncertain descriptions of the first practical telescope which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hans_Lippershey" title="Hans Lippershey">Hans Lippershey</a> tried to patent in the Netherlands in 1608,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKing200330–32_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKing200330–32-44">[44]</a></sup> Galileo, in the following year, made a telescope with about 3x magnification. He later made improved versions with up to about 30x magnification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1990133–134_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1990133–134-45">[45]</a></sup> With a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galilean_telescope" title="Galilean telescope">Galilean telescope</a>, the observer could see magnified, upright images on the Earth—it was what is commonly known as a terrestrial telescope or a spyglass. He could also use it to observe the sky; for a time he was one of those who could construct telescopes good enough for that purpose. On 25 August 1609, he demonstrated one of his early telescopes, with a magnification of about 8 or 9, to <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetian</a> lawmakers. His telescopes were also a profitable sideline for Galileo, who sold them to merchants who found them useful both at sea and as items of trade. He published his initial telescopic astronomical observations in March 1610 in a brief <a href="/wiki/Treatise" title="Treatise">treatise</a> entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Sidereus_Nuncius" title="Sidereus Nuncius">Sidereus Nuncius</a></i> (<i>Starry Messenger</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt19941–2_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt19941–2-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sidereus,_nuncius_magna_longeqve_admirabilia_spectacula..._Wellcome_L0072633.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5939" data-file-width="3539" decoding="async" height="285" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sidereus%2C_nuncius_magna_longeqve_admirabilia_spectacula..._Wellcome_L0072633.jpg/170px-Sidereus%2C_nuncius_magna_longeqve_admirabilia_spectacula..._Wellcome_L0072633.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sidereus%2C_nuncius_magna_longeqve_admirabilia_spectacula..._Wellcome_L0072633.jpg/255px-Sidereus%2C_nuncius_magna_longeqve_admirabilia_spectacula..._Wellcome_L0072633.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Sidereus%2C_nuncius_magna_longeqve_admirabilia_spectacula..._Wellcome_L0072633.jpg/340px-Sidereus%2C_nuncius_magna_longeqve_admirabilia_spectacula..._Wellcome_L0072633.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sidereus,_nuncius_magna_longeqve_admirabilia_spectacula..._Wellcome_L0072633.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the Moon from <i>Sidereus Nuncius</i>, published in Venice, 1610</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Moon">Moon</span></h4>
<p>On 30 November 1609, Galileo aimed his telescope at the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdgerton2009159_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdgerton2009159-47">[47]</a></sup> While not being the first person to observe the Moon through a telescope (English mathematician <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Harriot" title="Thomas Harriot">Thomas Harriot</a> had done it four months before but only saw a "strange spottednesse"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEdgerton2009155_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEdgerton2009155-48">[48]</a></sup> Galileo was the first to deduce the cause of the uneven waning as light occlusion from lunar mountains and <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">craters</a>. In his study, he also made topographical charts, estimating the heights of the mountains. The Moon was not what was long thought to have been a translucent and perfect sphere, as Aristotle claimed, and hardly the first "planet", an "eternal pearl to magnificently ascend into the heavenly empyrian", as put forth by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dante" title="Dante">Dante</a>. Galileo is sometimes credited with the discovery of the <a href="/wiki/Libration#Lunar_libration" title="Libration">lunar libration in latitude</a> in 1632,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> although Thomas Harriot or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(astronomer)" title="William Gilbert (astronomer)">William Gilbert</a> might have done it before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p><p>A friend of Galileo's, the painter Cigoli, included a realistic depiction of the Moon in one of his paintings, though probably used his own telescope to make the observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Jupiter.27s_moons"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter's_moons">Jupiter's moons</span></h4>
<p>On 7 January 1610, Galileo observed with his telescope what he described at the time as "three fixed stars, totally invisible<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[a]</a></sup> by their smallness", all close to Jupiter, and lying on a straight line through it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978146_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978146-52">[51]</a></sup> Observations on subsequent nights showed that the positions of these "stars" relative to Jupiter were changing in a way that would have been inexplicable if they had really been <a href="/wiki/Fixed_stars" title="Fixed stars">fixed stars</a>. On 10 January, Galileo noted that one of them had disappeared, an observation which he attributed to its being hidden behind Jupiter. Within a few days, he concluded that they were <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbiting</a> Jupiter: he had discovered <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">three of Jupiter's four largest moons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978152_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978152-53">[52]</a></sup> He discovered the fourth on 13 January. Galileo named the group of four the <i>Medicean stars</i>, in honour of his future patron, <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_II_de%27_Medici,_Grand_Duke_of_Tuscany" title="Cosimo II de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany">Cosimo II de' Medici, Grand Duke of Tuscany</a>, and Cosimo's three brothers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt199417_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt199417-54">[53]</a></sup> Later astronomers, however, renamed them <i><a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">Galilean satellites</a></i> in honour of their discoverer. These satellites were independently discovered by <a href="/wiki/Simon_Marius" title="Simon Marius">Simon Marius</a> on 8 January 1610 and are now called <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, the names given by Marius in his <i>Mundus Iovialis</i> published in 1614.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo's observations of the satellites of Jupiter caused a revolution in astronomy: a planet with smaller planets orbiting it did not conform to the principles of <a href="/wiki/On_the_Heavens" title="On the Heavens">Aristotelian cosmology</a>, which held that all heavenly bodies should circle the Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELinton200498,_205_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinton200498,_205-56">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978157_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978157-57">[56]</a></sup> and many astronomers and philosophers initially refused to believe that Galileo could have discovered such a thing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978158–168_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978158–168-58">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt199418–19_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt199418–19-59">[58]</a></sup> His observations were confirmed by the observatory of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Clavius" title="Christopher Clavius">Christopher Clavius</a> and he received a hero's welcome when he visited Rome in 1611.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannam2009313_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannam2009313-60">[59]</a></sup> Galileo continued to observe the satellites over the next eighteen months, and by mid-1611, he had obtained remarkably accurate estimates for their periods—a feat which <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> had believed impossible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978168_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978168-61">[60]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt199493_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt199493-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Phases_of_Venus">Phases of Venus</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phases-of-Venus.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/220px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/330px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Phases-of-Venus.svg/440px-Phases-of-Venus.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phases-of-Venus.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_Venus" title="Phases of Venus">phases of Venus</a>, observed by Galileo in 1610</div></div></div>
<p>From September 1610, Galileo observed that <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> exhibits <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_Venus" title="Phases of Venus">a full set of phases</a> similar to <a href="/wiki/Lunar_phase" title="Lunar phase">that of the Moon</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliocentric_model" title="Heliocentric model">heliocentric model</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> developed by <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> predicted that all phases would be visible since the orbit of Venus around the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> would cause its illuminated hemisphere to face the Earth when it was on the opposite side of the Sun and to face away from the Earth when it was on the Earth-side of the Sun. In <a href="/wiki/Geocentric_model#Ptolemaic_model" title="Geocentric model">Ptolemy's geocentric model</a>, it was impossible for any of the planets' orbits to intersect the spherical shell carrying the Sun. Traditionally, the orbit of Venus was placed entirely on the near side of the Sun, where it could exhibit only crescent and new phases. It was also possible to place it entirely on the far side of the Sun, where it could exhibit only gibbous and full phases. After Galileo's telescopic observations of the crescent, gibbous and full phases of Venus, the Ptolemaic model became untenable. In the early 17th century, as a result of his discovery, the great majority of astronomers converted to one of the various geo-heliocentric planetary models,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEThoren19898_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEThoren19898-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoskin1999117_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoskin1999117-64">[63]</a></sup> such as the <a href="/wiki/Tychonic_system" title="Tychonic system">Tychonic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Martianus_Capella" title="Martianus Capella">Capellan</a> and Extended Capellan models,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[b]</a></sup> each either with or without a daily rotating Earth. These all explained the phases of Venus without the 'refutation' of full heliocentrism's prediction of stellar parallax. Galileo's discovery of the phases of Venus was thus his most empirically practically influential contribution to the two-stage transition from full geocentrism to full heliocentrism via geo-heliocentrism.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn_and_Neptune">Saturn and Neptune</span></h4>
<p>In 1610, Galileo also observed the planet <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, and at first mistook its rings for planets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-66">[64]</a></sup> thinking it was a three-bodied system. When he observed the planet later, Saturn's rings were directly oriented at Earth, causing him to think that two of the bodies had disappeared. The rings reappeared when he observed the planet in 1616, further confusing him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo observed the planet <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> in 1612. It appears in his notebooks as one of many unremarkable dim stars. He did not realise that it was a planet, but he did note its motion relative to the stars before losing track of it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrakeKowal1980_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrakeKowal1980-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sunspots">Sunspots</span></h4>
<p>Galileo made naked-eye and telescopic studies of <a href="/wiki/Sunspot" title="Sunspot">sunspots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-69">[67]</a></sup> Their existence raised another difficulty with the unchanging perfection of the heavens as posited in orthodox Aristotelian celestial physics. An apparent annual variation in their trajectories, observed by <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Sizzi" title="Francesco Sizzi">Francesco Sizzi</a> and others in 1612–1613,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978209_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978209-70">[68]</a></sup> also provided a powerful argument against both the Ptolemaic system and the geoheliocentric system of Tycho Brahe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[c]</a></sup> A dispute over claimed priority in the discovery of sunspots, and in their interpretation, led Galileo to a long and bitter feud with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuit</a> <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Scheiner" title="Christoph Scheiner">Christoph Scheiner</a>. In the middle was <a href="/wiki/Mark_Welser" title="Mark Welser">Mark Welser</a>, to whom Scheiner had announced his discovery, and who asked Galileo for his opinion. Both of them were unaware of <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Fabricius" title="Johannes Fabricius">Johannes Fabricius</a>' earlier observation and publication of sunspots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGribbin200840_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGribbin200840-75">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Milky_Way_and_stars">Milky Way and stars</span></h4>
<p>Galileo observed the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, previously believed to be <a href="/wiki/Nebula" title="Nebula">nebulous</a>, and found it to be a multitude of stars packed so densely that they appeared from Earth to be clouds. He located many other stars too distant to be visible with the naked eye. He observed the double star <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mizar_(star)" title="Mizar (star)">Mizar</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ursa_Major" title="Ursa Major">Ursa Major</a> in 1617.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOndra200472–73_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOndra200472–73-76">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i>Starry Messenger</i>, Galileo reported that stars appeared as mere blazes of light, essentially unaltered in appearance by the telescope, and contrasted them to planets, which the telescope revealed to be discs. But shortly thereafter, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Letters_on_Sunspots" title="Letters on Sunspots">Letters on Sunspots</a></i>, he reported that the telescope revealed the shapes of both stars and planets to be "quite round". From that point forward, he continued to report that telescopes showed the roundness of stars, and that stars seen through the telescope measured a few seconds of arc in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraney2010455_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraney2010455-77">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraneyGrayson2011353_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraneyGrayson2011353-78">[75]</a></sup> He also devised a method for measuring the apparent size of a star without a telescope. As described in his <i><a href="/wiki/Dialogue_Concerning_the_Two_Chief_World_Systems" title="Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems">Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</a></i>, his method was to hang a thin rope in his line of sight to the star and measure the maximum distance from which it would wholly obscure the star. From his measurements of this distance and of the width of the rope, he could calculate the angle subtended by the star at his viewing point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVan_Helden198575_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVan_Helden198575-79">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChalmers199925_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChalmers199925-80">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalilei1953361–362_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalilei1953361–362-81">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his <i>Dialogue</i>, he reported that he had found the apparent diameter of a star of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stellar_magnitude" title="Stellar magnitude">first magnitude</a> to be no more than 5 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arcsecond" title="Arcsecond">arcseconds</a>, and that of one of sixth magnitude to be about <sup>5</sup>/<sub>6</sub> arcseconds. Like most astronomers of his day, Galileo did not recognise that the apparent sizes of stars that he measured were spurious, caused by diffraction and atmospheric distortion, and did not represent the true sizes of stars. However, Galileo's values were much smaller than previous estimates of the apparent sizes of the brightest stars, such as those made by Brahe, and enabled Galileo to counter anti-Copernican arguments such as those made by Tycho that these stars would have to be absurdly large for their annual <a href="/wiki/Stellar_parallax" title="Stellar parallax">parallaxes</a> to be undetectable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989167–176_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989167–176-82">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalilei1953359–360_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalilei1953359–360-83">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOndra200474–75_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOndra200474–75-84">[81]</a></sup> Other astronomers such as Simon Marius, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Riccioli" title="Giovanni Battista Riccioli">Giovanni Battista Riccioli</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Martin_van_den_Hove" title="Martin van den Hove">Martinus Hortensius</a> made similar measurements of stars, and Marius and Riccioli concluded the smaller sizes were not small enough to answer Tycho's argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraney2010454–462_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraney2010454–462-85">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraneyGrayson2011352–355_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraneyGrayson2011352–355-86">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_tides">Theory of tides</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="886" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg/180px-Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg/270px-Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg/360px-Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_Galilei_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galileo Galilei, portrait by <a href="/wiki/Domenico_Tintoretto" title="Domenico Tintoretto">Domenico Tintoretto</a></div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cardinal_Bellarmine" title="Cardinal Bellarmine">Cardinal Bellarmine</a> had written in 1615 that the <a href="/wiki/Copernican_heliocentrism" title="Copernican heliocentrism">Copernican system</a> could not be defended without "a true physical demonstration that the sun does not circle the earth but the earth circles the sun".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro198967–69_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro198967–69-87">[84]</a></sup> Galileo considered his theory of the <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a> to provide such evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[85]</a></sup> This theory was so important to him that he originally intended to call his <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i> the <i>Dialogue on the Ebb and Flow of the Sea</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989354_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989354-89">[86]</a></sup> The reference to tides was removed from the title by order of the Inquisition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>For Galileo, the tides were caused by the sloshing back and forth of water in the seas as a point on the Earth's surface sped up and slowed down because of the Earth's rotation on its axis and revolution around the Sun. He circulated his first account of the tides in 1616, addressed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alessandro_Orsini_(cardinal)" title="Alessandro Orsini (cardinal)">Cardinal Orsini</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989119–133_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989119–133-90">[87]</a></sup> His theory gave the first insight into the importance of the shapes of ocean basins in the size and timing of tides; he correctly accounted, for instance, for the negligible tides halfway along the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a> compared to those at the ends. As a general account of the cause of tides, however, his theory was a failure.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>If this theory were correct, there would be only one high tide per day. Galileo and his contemporaries were aware of this inadequacy because there are two daily high tides at <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venice</a> instead of one, about 12 hours apart. Galileo dismissed this anomaly as the result of several secondary causes including the shape of the sea, its depth, and other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989127–131_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989127–131-91">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalilei1953432–436_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalilei1953432–436-92">[89]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> later expressed the opinion that Galileo developed his "fascinating arguments" and accepted them uncritically out of a desire for physical proof of the motion of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinstein1953xvii_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinstein1953xvii-93">[90]</a></sup> Galileo also dismissed the idea, <a href="/wiki/Tide#History" title="Tide">known from antiquity</a> and by his contemporary Johannes Kepler, that the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalilei1953462_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalilei1953462-94">[91]</a></sup> caused the tides—Galileo also took no interest in Kepler's <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">elliptical orbits of the planets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[93]</a></sup> Galileo continued to argue in favour of his theory of tides, considering it the ultimate proof of Earth's motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[94]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy_over_comets_and_The_Assayer">Controversy over comets and <i>The Assayer</i></span></h3>
<p>In 1619, Galileo became embroiled in a controversy with Father <a href="/wiki/Orazio_Grassi" title="Orazio Grassi">Orazio Grassi</a>, professor of mathematics at the Jesuit <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Collegio_Romano" title="Collegio Romano">Collegio Romano</a>. It began as a dispute over the nature of comets, but by the time Galileo had published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Assayer" title="The Assayer">The Assayer</a></i> (<i>Il Saggiatore</i>) in 1623, his last salvo in the dispute, it had become a much wider controversy over the very nature of science itself. The title page of the book describes Galileo as philosopher and "Matematico Primario" of the Grand Duke of Tuscany.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Because <i>The Assayer</i> contains such a wealth of Galileo's ideas on how science should be practised, it has been referred to as his scientific manifesto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1960vii,_xxiii–xxiv_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1960vii,_xxiii–xxiv-98">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994139–140_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994139–140-99">[96]</a></sup> Early in 1619, Father Grassi had anonymously published a pamphlet, <i>An Astronomical Disputation on the Three Comets of the Year 1618</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrassi1960a_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrassi1960a-100">[97]</a></sup> which discussed the nature of a comet that had appeared late in November of the previous year. Grassi concluded that the comet was a fiery body that had moved along a segment of a great circle at a constant distance from the earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978268_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978268-101">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrassi1960a16)_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrassi1960a16)-102">[99]</a></sup> and since it moved in the sky more slowly than the Moon, it must be farther away than the Moon.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Grassi's arguments and conclusions were criticised in a subsequent article, <i><a href="/wiki/Discourse_on_Comets" title="Discourse on Comets">Discourse on Comets</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalileiGuiducci1960_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalileiGuiducci1960-103">[100]</a></sup> published under the name of one of Galileo's disciples, a Florentine lawyer named <a href="/wiki/Mario_Guiducci" title="Mario Guiducci">Mario Guiducci</a>, although it had been largely written by Galileo himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xvi_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xvi-104">[101]</a></sup> Galileo and Guiducci offered no definitive theory of their own on the nature of comets,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1957222_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1957222-105">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xvii_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xvii-106">[103]</a></sup> although they did present some tentative conjectures that are now known to be mistaken. (The correct approach to the study of comets had been proposed at the time by Tycho Brahe.) In its opening passage, Galileo and Guiducci's <i>Discourse</i> gratuitously insulted the Jesuit <a href="/wiki/Christoph_Scheiner" title="Christoph Scheiner">Christoph Scheiner</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994135_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994135-107">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xii_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xii-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalileiGuiducci196024_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalileiGuiducci196024-109">[106]</a></sup> and various uncomplimentary remarks about the professors of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_College" title="Roman College">Collegio Romano</a> were scattered throughout the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994135_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994135-107">[104]</a></sup> The Jesuits were offended,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994135_107-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994135-107">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xvii_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xvii-106">[103]</a></sup> and Grassi soon replied with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polemical" title="Polemical">polemical</a> tract of his own, <i>The Astronomical and Philosophical Balance</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrassi1960b_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrassi1960b-110">[107]</a></sup> under the pseudonym Lothario Sarsio Sigensano,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978494_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978494-111">[108]</a></sup> purporting to be one of his own pupils.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><i>The Assayer</i> was Galileo's devastating reply to the <i>Astronomical Balance</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalileo1960_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalileo1960-112">[109]</a></sup> It has been widely recognized as a masterpiece of polemical literature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994137_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994137-113">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1957227_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1957227-114">[111]</a></sup> in which "Sarsi's" arguments are subjected to withering scorn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994138–142_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994138–142-115">[112]</a></sup> It was greeted with wide acclaim, and particularly pleased the new pope, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_VIII" title="Pope Urban VIII">Urban VIII</a>, to whom it had been dedicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xix_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1960xix-116">[113]</a></sup> In Rome, in the previous decade, Barberini, the future Urban VIII, had come down on the side of Galileo and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lincean_Academy" title="Lincean Academy">Lincean Academy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo's dispute with Grassi permanently alienated many Jesuits,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1960vii_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1960vii-118">[115]</a></sup> and Galileo and his friends were convinced that they were responsible for bringing about his later condemnation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994175_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994175-119">[116]</a></sup> although supporting evidence for this is not conclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994175–178_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994175–178-120">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackwell200630_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackwell200630-121">[118]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversy_over_heliocentrism">Controversy over heliocentrism</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">Galileo affair</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1567" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg/220px-Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg/330px-Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg/440px-Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo_facing_the_Roman_Inquisition.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cristiano_Banti" title="Cristiano Banti">Cristiano Banti</a>'s 1857 painting <i>Galileo facing the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Inquisition" title="Roman Inquisition">Roman Inquisition</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>At the time of Galileo's conflict with the Church, the majority of educated people subscribed to the <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotelian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geocentric" title="Geocentric">geocentric</a> view that the Earth is the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_center_of_the_Universe" title="History of the center of the Universe">center of the Universe</a> and the orbit of all heavenly bodies, or Tycho Brahe's new system blending geocentrism with heliocentrism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHannam2009303–316_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHannam2009303–316-122">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup> Opposition to heliocentrism and Galileo's writings on it combined religious and scientific objections. Religious opposition to heliocentrism arose from biblical passages implying the fixed nature of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bible_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-bible-125">[d]</a></sup> Scientific opposition came from Brahe, who argued that if heliocentrism were true, an annual stellar parallax should be observed, though none was at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[e]</a></sup> Aristarchus and Copernicus had correctly postulated that parallax was negligible because the stars were so distant. However, Tycho countered that since stars <a href="/wiki/Airy_disk" title="Airy disk">appear to have measurable angular size</a>, if the stars were that distant and their apparent size is due to their physical size, they would be far larger than the Sun. In fact, <a href="/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)#History" title="Magnitude (astronomy)">it is not possible to observe the physical size of distant stars</a> without modern telescopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraneyDanielson2014_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraneyDanielson2014-128">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[f]</a></sup>
</p><p>Galileo defended heliocentrism based on <a href="/wiki/Sidereus_Nuncius" title="Sidereus Nuncius">his astronomical observations of 1609</a>. In December 1613, the Grand Duchess <a href="/wiki/Christina_of_Lorraine" title="Christina of Lorraine">Christina of Florence</a> confronted one of Galileo's friends and followers, <a href="/wiki/Benedetto_Castelli" title="Benedetto Castelli">Benedetto Castelli</a>, with biblical objections to the motion of the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[g]</a></sup> Prompted by this incident, Galileo wrote a <a href="/wiki/Letter_to_Benedetto_Castelli" title="Letter to Benedetto Castelli">letter to Castelli</a> in which he argued that heliocentrism was actually not contrary to biblical texts, and that the Bible was an authority on faith and morals, not science. This letter was not published, but circulated widely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro198927–28_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro198927–28-130">[124]</a></sup> Two years later, Galileo wrote a <a href="/wiki/Letter_to_the_Grand_Duchess_Christina" title="Letter to the Grand Duchess Christina">letter to Christina</a> that expanded his arguments previously made in eight pages to forty pages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989-132">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>By 1615, Galileo's writings on heliocentrism had been submitted to the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Inquisition" title="Roman Inquisition">Roman Inquisition</a> by Father <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Lorini" title="Niccolò Lorini">Niccolò Lorini</a>, who claimed that Galileo and his followers were attempting to reinterpret the Bible,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bible_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-bible-125">[d]</a></sup> which was seen as a violation of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Trent" title="Council of Trent">Council of Trent</a> and looked dangerously like <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELangford199256–57_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELangford199256–57-133">[126]</a></sup> Lorini specifically cited Galileo's letter to Castelli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro198928,_134_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro198928,_134-134">[127]</a></sup> Galileo went to Rome to defend himself and his ideas. At the start of 1616, Monsignor <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Ingoli" title="Francesco Ingoli">Francesco Ingoli</a> initiated a debate with Galileo, sending him an essay disputing the Copernican system. Galileo later stated that he believed this essay to have been instrumental in the action against Copernicanism that followed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraney201568–69_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraney201568–69-135">[128]</a></sup> Ingoli may have been commissioned by the Inquisition to write an expert opinion on the controversy, with the essay providing the basis for the Inquisition's actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro201072_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro201072-136">[129]</a></sup> The essay focused on eighteen physical and mathematical arguments against heliocentrism. It borrowed primarily from Tycho Brahe's arguments, notably that heliocentrism would require the stars as they appeared to be much larger than the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[h]</a></sup> The essay also included four theological arguments, but Ingoli suggested Galileo focus on the physical and mathematical arguments, and he did not mention Galileo's biblical ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGraney201566–76,_164–175,_187–195_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGraney201566–76,_164–175,_187–195-139">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>In February 1616, an Inquisitorial commission declared heliocentrism to be "foolish and absurd in philosophy, and formally heretical since it explicitly contradicts in many places the sense of Holy Scripture". The Inquisition found that the idea of the Earth's movement "receives the same judgement in philosophy and ... in regard to theological truth it is at least erroneous in faith".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wcumf_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-wcumf-140">[132]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pope_Paul_V" title="Pope Paul V">Pope Paul V</a> instructed Cardinal Bellarmine to deliver this finding to Galileo, and to order him to abandon heliocentrism. On 26 February, Galileo was called to Bellarmine's residence and ordered "to abandon completely ... the opinion that the sun stands still at the center of the world and the Earth moves, and henceforth not to hold, teach, or defend it in any way whatever, either orally or in writing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2010218_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2010218-141">[133]</a></sup> The decree of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congregation_of_the_Index" title="Congregation of the Index">Congregation of the Index</a> banned Copernicus's <i>De Revolutionibus</i> and other heliocentric works until correction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2010218_141-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2010218-141">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>For the next decade, Galileo stayed well away from the controversy. He revived his project of writing a book on the subject, encouraged by the election of Cardinal Maffeo <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barberini" title="Barberini">Barberini</a> as <a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_VIII" title="Pope Urban VIII">Pope Urban VIII</a> in 1623. Barberini was a friend and admirer of Galileo, and had opposed the admonition of Galileo in 1616. Galileo's resulting book, <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i>, was published in 1632, with formal authorization from the Inquisition and papal permission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pope_Urban_VIII_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pope_Urban_VIII-142">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo-sustermans4.jpg"><img alt="Justus Sustermans - Portrait of Galileo Galilei (Uffizi).jpg" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1092" data-file-width="1456" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Galileo-sustermans4.jpg/220px-Galileo-sustermans4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Galileo-sustermans4.jpg/330px-Galileo-sustermans4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Galileo-sustermans4.jpg/440px-Galileo-sustermans4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo-sustermans4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Galileo Galilei by <a href="/wiki/Justus_Sustermans" title="Justus Sustermans">Justus Sustermans</a>, 1636. <a href="/wiki/Uffizi" title="Uffizi">Uffizi Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Earlier, Pope Urban VIII had personally asked Galileo to give arguments for and against heliocentrism in the book, and to be careful not to advocate heliocentrism. Whether unknowingly or deliberately, Simplicio, the defender of the Aristotelian geocentric view in <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i>, was often caught in his own errors and sometimes came across as a fool. Indeed, although Galileo states in the preface of his book that the character is named after a famous Aristotelian philosopher (<a href="/wiki/Simplicius_of_Cilicia" title="Simplicius of Cilicia">Simplicius</a> in Latin, "Simplicio" in Italian), the name "Simplicio" in Italian also has the connotation of "simpleton".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro199782_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro199782-143">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMossWallace200311_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMossWallace200311-144">[136]</a></sup> This portrayal of Simplicio made <i>Dialogue Concerning the Two Chief World Systems</i> appear as an advocacy book: an attack on Aristotelian geocentrism and defence of the Copernican theory.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Most historians agree Galileo did not act out of malice and felt blindsided by the reaction to his book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[i]</a></sup> However, the Pope did not take the suspected public ridicule lightly, nor the Copernican advocacy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Galileo had alienated one of his biggest and most powerful supporters, the Pope, and was called to Rome to defend his writings<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[140]</a></sup> in September 1632. He finally arrived in February 1633 and was brought before inquisitor <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Maculani" title="Vincenzo Maculani">Vincenzo Maculani</a> to be <a href="/wiki/Criminal_charge" title="Criminal charge">charged</a>. Throughout his trial, Galileo steadfastly maintained that since 1616 he had faithfully kept his promise not to hold any of the condemned opinions, and initially he denied even defending them. However, he was eventually persuaded to admit that, contrary to his true intention, a reader of his <i>Dialogue</i> could well have obtained the impression that it was intended to be a defence of Copernicanism. In view of Galileo's rather implausible denial that he had ever held Copernican ideas after 1616 or ever intended to defend them in the <i>Dialogue</i>, his final interrogation, in July 1633, concluded with his being threatened with torture if he did not tell the truth, but he maintained his denial despite the threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994171–175_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994171–175-150">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2010308–317_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2010308–317-151">[142]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGingerich1992117–118_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGingerich1992117–118-152">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sentence of the Inquisition was delivered on 22 June. It was in three essential parts:
</p>
<ul><li>Galileo was found "vehemently suspect of heresy" (though he was never formally charged with heresy, relieving him of facing corporal punishment),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[144]</a></sup> namely of having held the opinions that the Sun lies motionless at the centre of the universe, that the Earth is not at its centre and moves, and that one may hold and defend an opinion as probable after it has been declared contrary to Holy Scripture. He was required to "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abjure" title="Abjure">abjure</a>, curse and detest" those opinions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFantoli2005139_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFantoli2005139-154">[145]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989288–293_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro1989288–293-155">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFantoli2005140_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFantoli2005140-156">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2005282–284_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2005282–284-157">[148]</a></sup></li>
<li>He was sentenced to formal imprisonment at the pleasure of the Inquisition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro198938,_291,_306_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFinocchiaro198938,_291,_306-158">[149]</a></sup> On the following day, this was commuted to house arrest, under which he remained for the rest of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[150]</a></sup></li>
<li>His offending <i>Dialogue</i> was banned; and in an action not announced at the trial, publication of any of his works was forbidden, including any he might write in the future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978367_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978367-160">[151]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESharratt1994184_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharratt1994184-161">[152]</a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:E_pur_si_muove.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1780" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/E_pur_si_muove.jpg/220px-E_pur_si_muove.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/E_pur_si_muove.jpg/330px-E_pur_si_muove.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/E_pur_si_muove.jpg/440px-E_pur_si_muove.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:E_pur_si_muove.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait, originally attributed to Murillo, of Galileo gazing at the words "E pur si muove" (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E_pur_si_muove!" title="E pur si muove!">And yet it moves</a></i>) (not legible in this image) scratched on the wall of his prison cell. The attribution and narrative surrounding the painting have since been contested.</div></div></div>
<p>According to popular legend, after recanting his theory that the Earth moved around the Sun, Galileo allegedly muttered the rebellious phrase "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E_pur_si_muove!" title="E pur si muove!">And yet it moves</a>". There was a claim that a 1640s painting by the Spanish painter <a href="/wiki/Bartolom%C3%A9_Esteban_Murillo" title="Bartolomé Esteban Murillo">Bartolomé Esteban Murillo</a> or an artist of his school, in which the words were hidden until restoration work in 1911, depicts an imprisoned Galileo apparently gazing at the words "E pur si muove" written on the wall of his dungeon. The earliest known written account of the legend dates to a century after his death. Based on the painting, <a href="/wiki/Stillman_Drake" title="Stillman Drake">Stillman Drake</a> wrote  "there is no doubt now that the famous words were already attributed to Galileo before his death".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDrake1978356–357_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDrake1978356–357-162">[153]</a></sup> However, an intensive investigation by astrophysicist <a href="/wiki/Mario_Livio" title="Mario Livio">Mario Livio</a> has revealed that said painting is most probably a copy of a 1837 painting by the Flemish painter Roman-Eugene Van Maldeghem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[154]</a></sup>
